قوله : (الذي تجاوز هذا بالزاي المعجمة أو بالراء المهملة المجاوزة أو المجاورة والمآل واحد لأن الموضعين الذين يكونان تحت قطبي المعدل ومنطقة البروج لم يكونا متباعدين غاية التباعد وقوله : لم تصل أي ولم تصل تلك المواضع التي تجاوز هذه المواضع المذكورة إلى قطب العالم فكانت هي بين بين وحاصل الكلام هاهنا أن منطقة البروج في المواضع التي تجاور هذه المواضع هي المذكورة ولم تصل إلى قطب العالم، تنقسم هي على أربع قسي بحيث يكون كل قوس نقطة من النقط الأربع التي هي ! الاعتدالان والانقلابان . فالقوس التي يتوسطها الانقلاب الصيفي به لا تغيب أبداً بالنسبة إلى تلك المواضع، والقوس التي يتوسطها الانقلاب الشتوي لا تطلع أبداً بالنسبة إلى تلك المواضع أيضاً. والقوسان اللتان يتوسطهما الاعتدالان تطلع إحداهما مستقيمة وتغرب معوجة بالنسبة إلى تلك المواضع أيضاً . وتكون القوس الأخرى بالعكس على ما فصله. وقوله : يتوسطها الانقلاب صفة للقوس. وقوله : يتوسطهما صفة للقوسين. وقوله: يتوسطهما أول الميزان أي يتوسطها الاعتدال الخريفي. وقوله يتوسطها الحمل أي يتوسطها

قوله : (إما زائدة) لأن المقصود هاهنا هي المواضع المذكورة التي هي غير خط الاستواء
وهي أكثر من الثلاثة على ما ذكره المصنف .
The first observatory - the third section - the seventh objective: the daily orbits of the Earth. From one of the sun [they mean the sun's zenith], the heads are directly overhead twice, that is when it is in two points of the ecliptic whose declination equals the inclination of the equator from the zenith in that land, and likewise their seasons are eight. (Except that the seasons are not equal in duration, and perhaps the two points are very close to the solstices, so they are in their ruling, so the number of seasons decreases there and their summer becomes long. And in the places that are under the solstices, their heads are directly overhead once in the year, and their seasons are four equal. And beyond that, their heads are not directly overhead, but rather approach it in one of the solstices and move away from it in the other, and their seasons are those four. And in the places where the summer circle is ever-visible, the sun does not set there for one daily cycle, so the day becomes twenty-four hours, and this [cycle] is wherever the sun is at the summer solstice. And it is not hidden from you that in these places as well, the winter circle is ever-hidden, so the sun does not rise there for one cycle, but rather its duration is night, contrary to the first circle, so there is no need in that to consider other places, as he mentioned by his saying: 'And in the places where the summer circle is ever-hidden, the sun does not rise there for one cycle, so the night then becomes twenty-four hours.' However, [the phrase] 'the ever-hidden circle in another place' is not without weakness. And in the places where the pole of the ecliptic passes through their zenith, when its pole is at the zenith, the ecliptic coincides with the horizon, since its pole and the pole of the horizon (both are great circles on one sphere) become [one]; then if the pole (i.e., the pole of the ecliptic) moves by the motion of the whole towards the west, half of the eastern ecliptic rises above the horizon, and the western half descends from it all at once, since when the poles separate, the two great circles intersect at half. And know that the places where the summer circle is ever-visible and the winter circle ever-hidden are precisely the places where the pole of the ecliptic passes through their zenith. And in the places that go beyond these mentioned places and do not reach the pole of the world, there is an arc of the ecliptic centered on the summer solstice ever-visible, never setting, and another arc centered on the winter solstice ever-hidden, never rising, and between them, from both sides, two other arcs centered on the equinoxes: one of them, which is centered on the beginning of Libra if the visible pole is northern, and that which is centered on the beginning of Aries if the visible pole is southern, rises straight and sets crooked, i.e., the beginnings of the signs rise before their ends straightly, and their ends set before their beginnings crookedly; and the other arc [is] the opposite, i.e., rises crooked and sets straight. And in these three places' - the word 'three' is either redundant, or he meant by it what is between the equator and the solstices' circles, and what is under the solstices, and what is beyond that, and not... [commentary continues]. His saying: 'The two great circles intersect at half' i.e., they intersect not at right angles, for that is not conceivable here. And his saying: 'And know' etc. And his saying: 'And know' includes a critique of the author, for his expression suggests a difference between them while there is no difference at all. His saying: 'which goes beyond this' - with the dotted zay or with the unpointed ra, meaning 'adjoining' or 'adjacent', and the result is one, because the two places that are under the poles of the equator and the ecliptic are not extremely distant. And his saying: 'did not reach' i.e., those places that go beyond these mentioned places did not reach the pole of the world, so they were intermediate. The summary here is that the ecliptic in the places adjacent to these mentioned places and not reaching the pole of the world is divided into four arcs, such that each arc has a point among the four points which are the equinoxes and solstices. Thus the arc centered on the summer solstice never sets relative to those places, and the arc centered on the winter solstice never rises relative to those places also. And the two arcs centered on the equinoxes: one of them rises straight and sets crooked relative to those places also, and the other arc is the opposite as he detailed. And his saying: 'centered on the solstice' is a description of the arc. And his saying: 'centered on them' is a description of the two arcs. And his saying: 'centered on the beginning of Libra' means centered on the autumnal equinox. And his saying: 'centered on Aries' means centered on the vernal equinox. His saying: 'either redundant' because the intended here are the mentioned places which are other than the equator, and they are more than three as the author mentioned.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a continuous commentary on the seventh objective of the third section of the first observatory of al-Mawaqif, dealing with the daily orbits of the Earth and the resulting celestial phenomena. The commentator explains various astronomical cases concerning the sun's zenith and seasons. The passage begins by describing places where the sun becomes directly overhead twice a year, namely when the sun's declination equals the latitude of the location; in such places, the seasons are eight, though the commentator notes that the seasons are not equal in duration, and if the two overhead points are close to the solstices, the number of seasons effectively decreases and summer becomes longer. Next, the commentator discusses places under the solstices (i.e., at latitudes exactly equal to the obliquity), where the sun is overhead once a year, resulting in four equal seasons. Beyond these latitudes, the sun never becomes directly overhead but approaches during one solstice and recedes during the other; still the seasons are four. The passage then turns to polar regions: places where the summer circle is always visible experience a day of 24 hours, and correspondingly the winter circle is always hidden, yielding a 24-hour night. The commentator notes that these two phenomena occur in the same locations, so there is no need to consider separate places, and he critiques the author's phrasing regarding the 'ever-hidden circle'. The comment then addresses places where the pole of the ecliptic coincides with the zenith; in such places, the ecliptic aligns with the horizon, and as the pole moves, half the ecliptic rises and half sets simultaneously. The commentator clarifies that the places with ever-visible and ever-hidden circles are precisely those where the ecliptic pole is at the zenith. Further, in intermediate latitudes between these and the pole, the ecliptic is divided into four arcs: one centered on the summer solstice never sets, one centered on the winter solstice never rises, and two arcs centered on the equinoxes exhibit opposite rising and setting behaviors (one rises straight and sets crooked, the other the reverse). The commentator explains that the word 'three' in 'these three places' is either redundant or refers to three general zones: between equator and tropics, under tropics, and beyond. Throughout, the commentator provides glosses and critical remarks on the author's wording, clarifying meanings and correcting potential ambiguities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the sun becomes directly overhead everywhere twice a year.
Correction: It only happens between the tropics; under the solstices it is once, and beyond it is never.

Checkpoint
In places under the solstices, how many times does the sun become directly overhead in a year?
A) Twice
B) Once
C) Never
D) Four times

Key Takeaway
The sun's overhead zenith occurs zero, one, or two times per year depending on the latitude, directly affecting the number and equality of seasons.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the changing seasons throughout the year, do you recognise them as a direct result of the precise astronomical position of your location? How often do you pause to consider the exactness of the Creator's design in setting the sun's path?
?
The passage tells us that in some places the sun is overhead twice and seasons are eight and unequal. Does this diversity in creation make you feel more awe of the One who ordained it? What step can you take today to learn one more fact about the universe He created?
?
If the sun never becomes directly overhead where you live (as in many regions), does that make you feel distant from the source of light? Or does it remind you that even in that 'retreat' there is wisdom? What personal attitude change does this call for in your relationship with the natural signs of Allah?

Polar Day and Night: Ever-Visible and Ever-Hidden Circles
Describes polar day and night and the ever-visible/ever-hidden circles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the phenomena of continuous daylight and darkness at the poles and the underlying astronomical reasoning.

Core Idea
Near the poles, the sun may not set for a full day or may not rise at all.

The commentator now turns to the extreme regions near the poles, where the sun's daily circle becomes either entirely above or entirely below the horizon. He explains how a 24-hour day occurs when the summer circle is ever-visible, and a 24-hour night when the winter circle is ever-hidden, and that these occur in the same locations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage describing polar phenomena
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the terms 'ever-visible' and 'ever-hidden'.
The commentator uses parentheses to add remarks.
2
Understand the 24-hour day
The passage explains that in places where the summer circle is ever-visible, the sun does not set for a full daily cycle, resulting in a 24-hour day.
This happens at the summer solstice.
3
Understand the 24-hour night
In the same places, the winter circle is ever-hidden, so the sun does not rise for a cycle, giving a 24-hour night.
The commentator emphasizes they occur together.
4
Note the commentator's critique
The commentator criticizes the author's phrasing that implies separate places for the ever-hidden circle.
He calls it 'weak' (ركاكة).

The first observatory - the third section - the seventh objective: the daily orbits of the Earth. From one of the sun [they mean the sun's zenith], the heads are directly overhead twice, that is when it is in two points of the ecliptic whose declination equals the inclination of the equator from the zenith in that land, and likewise their seasons are eight. (Except that the seasons are not equal in duration, and perhaps the two points are very close to the solstices, so they are in their ruling, so the number of seasons decreases there and their summer becomes long. And in the places that are under the solstices, their heads are directly overhead once in the year, and their seasons are four equal. And beyond that, their heads are not directly overhead, but rather approach it in one of the solstices and move away from it in the other, and their seasons are those four. And in the places where the summer circle is ever-visible, the sun does not set there for one daily cycle, so the day becomes twenty-four hours, and this [cycle] is wherever the sun is at the summer solstice. And it is not hidden from you that in these places as well, the winter circle is ever-hidden, so the sun does not rise there for one cycle, but rather its duration is night, contrary to the first circle, so there is no need in that to consider other places, as he mentioned by his saying: 'And in the places where the summer circle is ever-hidden, the sun does not rise there for one cycle, so the night then becomes twenty-four hours.' However, [the phrase] 'the ever-hidden circle in another place' is not without weakness. And in the places where the pole of the ecliptic passes through their zenith, when its pole is at the zenith, the ecliptic coincides with the horizon, since its pole and the pole of the horizon (both are great circles on one sphere) become [one]; then if the pole (i.e., the pole of the ecliptic) moves by the motion of the whole towards the west, half of the eastern ecliptic rises above the horizon, and the western half descends from it all at once, since when the poles separate, the two great circles intersect at half. And know that the places where the summer circle is ever-visible and the winter circle ever-hidden are precisely the places where the pole of the ecliptic passes through their zenith. And in the places that go beyond these mentioned places and do not reach the pole of the world, there is an arc of the ecliptic centered on the summer solstice ever-visible, never setting, and another arc centered on the winter solstice ever-hidden, never rising, and between them, from both sides, two other arcs centered on the equinoxes: one of them, which is centered on the beginning of Libra if the visible pole is northern, and that which is centered on the beginning of Aries if the visible pole is southern, rises straight and sets crooked, i.e., the beginnings of the signs rise before their ends straightly, and their ends set before their beginnings crookedly; and the other arc [is] the opposite, i.e., rises crooked and sets straight. And in these three places' - the word 'three' is either redundant, or he meant by it what is between the equator and the solstices' circles, and what is under the solstices, and what is beyond that, and not... [commentary continues]. His saying: 'The two great circles intersect at half' i.e., they intersect not at right angles, for that is not conceivable here. And his saying: 'And know' etc. And his saying: 'And know' includes a critique of the author, for his expression suggests a difference between them while there is no difference at all. His saying: 'which goes beyond this' - with the dotted zay or with the unpointed ra, meaning 'adjoining' or 'adjacent', and the result is one, because the two places that are under the poles of the equator and the ecliptic are not extremely distant. And his saying: 'did not reach' i.e., those places that go beyond these mentioned places did not reach the pole of the world, so they were intermediate. The summary here is that the ecliptic in the places adjacent to these mentioned places and not reaching the pole of the world is divided into four arcs, such that each arc has a point among the four points which are the equinoxes and solstices. Thus the arc centered on the summer solstice never sets relative to those places, and the arc centered on the winter solstice never rises relative to those places also. And the two arcs centered on the equinoxes: one of them rises straight and sets crooked relative to those places also, and the other arc is the opposite as he detailed. And his saying: 'centered on the solstice' is a description of the arc. And his saying: 'centered on them' is a description of the two arcs. And his saying: 'centered on the beginning of Libra' means centered on the autumnal equinox. And his saying: 'centered on Aries' means centered on the vernal equinox. Commentary
This passage is a continuous commentary on the seventh objective of the third section of the first observatory of al-Mawaqif, dealing with the daily orbits of the Earth and the resulting celestial phenomena. The commentator explains various astronomical cases concerning the sun's zenith and seasons. The passage begins by describing places where the sun becomes directly overhead twice a year, namely when the sun's declination equals the latitude of the location; in such places, the seasons are eight, though the commentator notes that the seasons are not equal in duration, and if the two overhead points are close to the solstices, the number of seasons effectively decreases and summer becomes longer. Next, the commentator discusses places under the solstices (i.e., at latitudes exactly equal to the obliquity), where the sun is overhead once a year, resulting in four equal seasons. Beyond these latitudes, the sun never becomes directly overhead but approaches during one solstice and recedes during the other; still the seasons are four. The passage then turns to polar regions: places where the summer circle is always visible experience a day of 24 hours, and correspondingly the winter circle is always hidden, yielding a 24-hour night. The commentator notes that these two phenomena occur in the same locations, so there is no need to consider separate places, and he critiques the author's phrasing regarding the 'ever-hidden circle'. The comment then addresses places where the pole of the ecliptic coincides with the zenith; in such places, the ecliptic aligns with the horizon, and as the pole moves, half the ecliptic rises and half sets simultaneously. The commentator clarifies that the places with ever-visible and ever-hidden circles are precisely those where the ecliptic pole is at the zenith. Further, in intermediate latitudes between these and the pole, the ecliptic is divided into four arcs: one centered on the summer solstice never sets, one centered on the winter solstice never rises, and two arcs centered on the equinoxes exhibit opposite rising and setting behaviors (one rises straight and sets crooked, the other the reverse). The commentator explains that the word 'three' in 'these three places' is either redundant or refers to three general zones: between equator and tropics, under tropics, and beyond. Throughout, the commentator provides glosses and critical remarks on the author's wording, clarifying meanings and correcting potential ambiguities.

Correction: They occur in the same polar regions.

Ecliptic Pole at Zenith and Arc Visibility Patterns
Describes ecliptic pole at zenith and arcs' visibility patterns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the ecliptic aligns with the horizon when its pole is at the zenith, and how arcs of the ecliptic behave in intermediate latitudes.

Core Idea
When the ecliptic pole is overhead, the ecliptic coincides with the horizon, and arcs can be ever-visible, ever-hidden, or have distinct rising/setting patterns.

The commentator moves to a special configuration where the pole of the ecliptic passes through the local zenith. This causes the ecliptic to align with the horizon, and as the pole moves, half rises and half sets. He then explains how, in latitudes between this and the world pole, the ecliptic is divided into four arcs with distinct rising and setting behaviors. This passage shows the complexity of celestial phenomena at different latitudes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on ecliptic pole at zenith
Read the Arabic and translation. Understand that when the ecliptic pole is at the zenith, the ecliptic coincides with the horizon.
The passage describes the motion of the pole causing half the ecliptic to rise and half to set.
2
Identify the connection to polar circles
The commentator notes that the places with ever-visible/ever-hidden circles are the same as where the ecliptic pole passes through the zenith.
This links the earlier polar day/night to this configuration.
3
Examine the four arcs in intermediate latitudes
For places beyond these but not reaching the world pole, the ecliptic has four arcs: two centered on solstices (ever-visible or ever-hidden) and two centered on equinoxes with opposite rising/setting behaviors.
Note the distinction between rising straight vs. crooked.
4
Reflect on the commentator's critique of 'three places'
The passage ends with a remark that the word 'three' may be redundant or refer to general zones.
This is a textual critique by the commentator.

The first observatory - the third section - the seventh objective: the daily orbits of the Earth. From one of the sun [they mean the sun's zenith], the heads are directly overhead twice, that is when it is in two points of the ecliptic whose declination equals the inclination of the equator from the zenith in that land, and likewise their seasons are eight. (Except that the seasons are not equal in duration, and perhaps the two points are very close to the solstices, so they are in their ruling, so the number of seasons decreases there and their summer becomes long. And in the places that are under the solstices, their heads are directly overhead once in the year, and their seasons are four equal. And beyond that, their heads are not directly overhead, but rather approach it in one of the solstices and move away from it in the other, and their seasons are those four. And in the places where the summer circle is ever-visible, the sun does not set there for one daily cycle, so the day becomes twenty-four hours, and this [cycle] is wherever the sun is at the summer solstice. And it is not hidden from you that in these places as well, the winter circle is ever-hidden, so the sun does not rise there for one cycle, but rather its duration is night, contrary to the first circle, so there is no need in that to consider other places, as he mentioned by his saying: 'And in the places where the summer circle is ever-hidden, the sun does not rise there for one cycle, so the night then becomes twenty-four hours.' However, [the phrase] 'the ever-hidden circle in another place' is not without weakness. And in the places where the pole of the ecliptic passes through their zenith, when its pole is at the zenith, the ecliptic coincides with the horizon, since its pole and the pole of the horizon (both are great circles on one sphere) become [one]; then if the pole (i.e., the pole of the ecliptic) moves by the motion of the whole towards the west, half of the eastern ecliptic rises above the horizon, and the western half descends from it all at once, since when the poles separate, the two great circles intersect at half. And know that the places where the summer circle is ever-visible and the winter circle ever-hidden are precisely the places where the pole of the ecliptic passes through their zenith. And in the places that go beyond these mentioned places and do not reach the pole of the world, there is an arc of the ecliptic centered on the summer solstice ever-visible, never setting, and another arc centered on the winter solstice ever-hidden, never rising, and between them, from both sides, two other arcs centered on the equinoxes: one of them, which is centered on the beginning of Libra if the visible pole is northern, and that which is centered on the beginning of Aries if the visible pole is southern, rises straight and sets crooked, i.e., the beginnings of the signs rise before their ends straightly, and their ends set before their beginnings crookedly; and the other arc [is] the opposite, i.e., rises crooked and sets straight. And in these three places' - the word 'three' is either redundant, or he meant by it what is between the equator and the solstices' circles, and what is under the solstices, and what is beyond that, and not... [commentary continues]. His saying: 'The two great circles intersect at half' i.e., they intersect not at right angles, for that is not conceivable here. And his saying: 'And know' etc. And his saying: 'And know' includes a critique of the author, for his expression suggests a difference between them while there is no difference at all. His saying: 'which goes beyond this' - with the dotted zay or with the unpointed ra, meaning 'adjoining' or 'adjacent', and the result is one, because the two places that are under the poles of the equator and the ecliptic are not extremely distant. And his saying: 'did not reach' i.e., those places that go beyond these mentioned places did not reach the pole of the world, so they were intermediate. The summary here is that the ecliptic in the places adjacent to these mentioned places and not reaching the pole of the world is divided into four arcs, such that each arc has a point among the four points which are the equinoxes and solstices. Thus the arc centered on the summer solstice never sets relative to those places, and the arc centered on the winter solstice never rises relative to those places also. And the two arcs centered on the equinoxes: one of them rises straight and sets crooked relative to those places also, and the other arc is the opposite as he detailed. And his saying: 'centered on the solstice' is a description of the arc. And his saying: 'centered on them' is a description of the two arcs. And his saying: 'centered on the beginning of Libra' means centered on the autumnal equinox. And his saying: 'centered on Aries' means centered on the vernal equinox. His saying: 'either redundant' because the intended here are the mentioned places which are other than the equator, and they are more than three as the author mentioned.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a continuous commentary on the seventh objective of the third section of the first observatory of al-Mawaqif, dealing with the daily orbits of the Earth and the resulting celestial phenomena. The commentator explains various astronomical cases concerning the sun's zenith and seasons. The passage begins by describing places where the sun becomes directly overhead twice a year, namely when the sun's declination equals the latitude of the location; in such places, the seasons are eight, though the commentator notes that the seasons are not equal in duration, and if the two overhead points are close to the solstices, the number of seasons effectively decreases and summer becomes longer. Next, the commentator discusses places under the solstices (i.e., at latitudes exactly equal to the obliquity), where the sun is overhead once a year, resulting in four equal seasons. Beyond these latitudes, the sun never becomes directly overhead but approaches during one solstice and recedes during the other; still the seasons are four. The passage then turns to polar regions: places where the summer circle is always visible experience a day of 24 hours, and correspondingly the winter circle is always hidden, yielding a 24-hour night. The commentator notes that these two phenomena occur in the same locations, so there is no need to consider separate places, and he critiques the author's phrasing regarding the 'ever-hidden circle'. The comment then addresses places where the pole of the ecliptic coincides with the zenith; in such places, the ecliptic aligns with the horizon, and as the pole moves, half the ecliptic rises and half sets simultaneously. The commentator clarifies that the places with ever-visible and ever-hidden circles are precisely those where the ecliptic pole is at the zenith. Further, in intermediate latitudes between these and the pole, the ecliptic is divided into four arcs: one centered on the summer solstice never sets, one centered on the winter solstice never rises, and two arcs centered on the equinoxes exhibit opposite rising and setting behaviors (one rises straight and sets crooked, the other the reverse). The commentator explains that the word 'three' in 'these three places' is either redundant or refers to three general zones: between equator and tropics, under tropics, and beyond. Throughout, the commentator provides glosses and critical remarks on the author's wording, clarifying meanings and correcting potential ambiguities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the ever-visible/ever-hidden circles occur in different locations from where the ecliptic pole is at zenith.
Correction: They are precisely the same locations.

Checkpoint

C) Both set straight
D) Both rise and set crooked

Key Takeaway
The ecliptic's position relative to the horizon determines patterns of continuous day/night and the specific rising and setting behaviors of its arcs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage describes how half the ecliptic rises and half sets all at once when the ecliptic pole is at the zenith. Is there a truth in your life that you have been trying to keep partially hidden, but it is actually rising all at once? How will you respond to this sudden exposure?
?
In intermediate places, arcs centered on the solstices are ever-visible or ever-hidden. Consider a virtue or a sin in your life that seems permanent—always visible or always hidden. What step can you take today to change its trajectory, realizing that your spiritual 'latitude' is not fixed?

The Four Arcs of the Ecliptic and Their Visibility Rules
Explains the division of the ecliptic into four arcs and their visibility rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the ecliptic is divided into four arcs based on the solstices and equinoxes, and how each arc behaves in terms of visibility and rising/setting at intermediate latitudes.

Core Idea
The ecliptic's arcs: one always visible, one always hidden, two with opposite rising/setting patterns.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif explains the classification of the ecliptic arcs in relation to latitude. The commentator clarifies the author's statements and provides a detailed breakdown of the arcs' visibility patterns. Understanding these arcs is crucial for grasping the geometry of the celestial sphere in Islamic astronomical traditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is dense with astronomical terms; focus on the overall classification.
2
Identify the four arcs
The passage describes four arcs: two centered on the solstices (one never sets, one never rises) and two centered on the equinoxes (one rises straight and sets crooked, the other does the opposite). Locate each description.
Note that the behavior depends on whether the pole of the ecliptic is north or south.
3
Understand the critique of 'three places'
The commentator argues that the author's mention of 'three places' is either redundant or refers to broader categories. This is a subtle textual criticism. Consider why the commentator makes this point.
This shows the commentator's careful reading of the original text.
4
Reflect on the geometry
Consider how the intersection of the ecliptic and horizon creates these arcs. The commentator mentions that the two great circles intersect not at right angles, which affects the arcs.
Visualizing the celestial sphere may help.

معوجة وتغرب مستقيمة. وفي هذه المواضع الثلاثة لفظة الثلاثة إما زائدة، أو أراد بها ما بين خط الاستواء ومدار الانقلابين وما تحت الانقلابين وما جاوز ذلك ولم يبلغ القطب. قوله: (تتقاطع العظيمتان على المعجمة أو بالراء المهملة المجاوزة أو المجاورة والمآل واحد لأن الموضعين الذين يكونان تحت قطبي المعدل ومنطقة البروج لم يكونا متباعدين غاية التباعد. وقوله: (لم تصل) أي لم تصل تلك المواضع التي تجاوز هذه المواضع المذكورة إلى قطب العالم فكانت هي بين بين. وحاصل الكلام هاهنا أن منطقة البروج في المواضع التي تجاور هذه المواضع المذكورة ولم تصل إلى قطب العالم تنقسم هي على أربع قسي بحيث يكون كل قوس بين نقطة من النقط الأربع التي هي الاعتدالان والانقلابان. فالقوس التي يتوسطها الانقلاب الصيفي لا تغيب أبداً بالنسبة إلى تلك المواضع، والقوس التي يتوسطها الانقلاب الشتوي لا تطلع أبداً بالنسبة إلى تلك المواضع أيضاً. والقوسان اللتان يتوسطهما الاعتدالان تطلع إحداهما مستقيمة وتغرب معوجة بالنسبة إلى تلك المواضع أيضاً، وتكون القوس الأخرى بالعكس على ما فصله. وقوله: (يتوسطها الانقلاب) صفة للقوس. وقوله: (يتوسطهما) صفة للقوسين. وقوله: (يتوسطهما أول الميزان) أي يتوسطها الاعتدال الخريفي. وقوله: (يتوسطها الحمل) أي يتوسطها الاعتدال الربيعي. قوله: (إما زائدة) لأن المقصود هاهنا هي المواضع المذكورة التي هي غير خط الاستواء وهي أكثر من الثلاثة على ما ذكره المصنف. وهو الخط.
The declination towards the west rose above the horizon the eastern half of the zone and declined from it the western half all at once, because in the state of separation of the two poles the two great circles intersect each other in half. And know that the places where the summer orbit is always visible and the winter orbit always hidden are exactly the places where the pole of the ecliptic passes through their zenith. And in the places that exceed these mentioned places and do not reach the pole of the world, a arc of the zone whose middle is the summer solstice is always visible, never setting, and another arc thereof whose middle is the winter solstice is always hidden, never rising. And between them from both sides are two other arcs whose middle are the equinoxes: one of them, which is the one whose middle is the beginning of Libra if the apparent pole is northern, and whose middle is the beginning of Aries if the apparent pole is southern, rises straight and sets crooked; that is, the beginnings of the signs rise before their ends in a straight manner, and their ends set before their beginnings in a crooked manner. And the other arc is the opposite, i.e., it rises crooked and sets straight. And in these three places, the word 'three' is either redundant or he meant by it the places between the equator and the tropics and under the tropics and what exceeds that and does not reach the pole. The commentator says: 'They intersect each other in half' meaning they intersect not at right angles because that is not conceivable here. And his statement 'And know etc.' contains a criticism of the author, for his wording suggests a difference between them while there is no difference at all. And his statement 'which exceeded this' with a dotted zay or an undotted ra', meaning the adjacent or neighboring, and the outcome is one because the two places that are under the poles of the equator and the ecliptic are not extremely far apart. And his statement 'did not reach' means those places that exceeded these mentioned places did not reach the pole of the world, so they were in-between. And the gist of the discourse here is that the ecliptic in the places adjacent to these mentioned places and not reaching the pole of the world is divided into four arcs such that each arc is between one of the four points which are the equinoxes and solstices. So the arc whose middle is the summer solstice never sets with respect to those places, and the arc whose middle is the winter solstice never rises with respect to those places as well. And the two arcs whose middle are the equinoxes, one of them rises straight and sets crooked with respect to those places as well, and the other arc is the opposite as he detailed. And his statement 'the solstice is its middle' is a description of the arc. And his statement 'both of them are their middle' is a description of the two arcs. And his statement 'the beginning of Libra is their middle' means the autumnal equinox is its middle. And his statement 'the beginning of Aries is its middle' means the vernal equinox is its middle. And his statement 'it is redundant' because the intended here are the mentioned places which are other than the equator and they are more than three according to what the author mentioned. And it is the line.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is an explanation (by the commentator) of the author's classification of the arcs of the ecliptic in relation to different latitudes. The commentator begins by clarifying a previous statement about the intersection of two great circles. Then he explains that the places where the summer orbit is always visible and winter always hidden are those where the pole of the ecliptic is at the zenith. For intermediate latitudes (neither at the equator nor at the pole), the ecliptic is divided into four arcs: the arc centered on the summer solstice is always visible (never sets), the arc centered on the winter solstice is always hidden (never rises), and the two arcs centered on the equinoxes have opposite behaviors depending on the hemisphere: one rises straight and sets crooked (when the center is the beginning of Libra for northern hemisphere, or Aries for southern), and the other does the reverse (rises crooked and sets straight). The commentator then critiques the author's use of 'three' places, suggesting it is either redundant or refers to three categories (between equator and tropics, under the tropics, and beyond). He goes on to explain the commentator's own remarks about the intersection, the meaning of 'exceeded', and the overall conclusion. This is a detailed astronomical classification using the ecliptic and horizon coordinates.

Day and Night at the Poles
Describes the daily motion and the annual cycle of day and night at the Earth's poles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the astronomical reasons for the length of day and night at the Earth's poles and how the sun's motion creates the polar day/night cycle.

Core Idea
At the poles, the year is one long day and one long night due to celestial geometry.

This passage from the commentary explains the unique astronomical conditions at the Earth's poles. It shows how the alignment of the celestial equator with the horizon prevents daily rising and setting of stars, and how the sun's annual path results in a gradual six-month rise and six-month set, creating a year consisting of one continuous day and one continuous night. Understanding this clarifies how the celestial model accounts for observed polar phenomena.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully to get an overview of the content. Notice the technical terms like 'حمائلية' and 'آفاق مائلة'.
The term 'حمائلية' refers to a specific type of daily motion; the context suggests it means the motion is such that the horizon is inclined.
2
Identify the key geometric condition
The passage states that at the poles, the celestial equator (المعدل) coincides with the horizon because the Earth's axis is perpendicular. This is the fundamental condition that drives all subsequent effects.
3
Understand the effect on daily motion
Because the equator is the horizon, the daily rotation of the celestial sphere does not cause any rising or setting of stars. Instead, stars only rise/set due to their own proper motions.
4
Trace the sun's annual altitude variation
The sun moves through the ecliptic, which is split: half always above the horizon, half always below. Over the year, the sun's altitude changes gradually, taking three months to reach maximum altitude (total declination) and three months to descend back to the horizon.
This results in six months of daylight and six months of night, but the passage notes that at the North Pole daytime is longer due to apogee/perigee.
5
Review the complete cycle
Combine all elements: the annual cycle consists of one long day and one long night, with the sun appearing to circle the sky at constant altitude, changing altitude only over months.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يبلغ القطب ( تكون الحركة اليومية حمائلية وتسمى آفاقها مائلة ( وحيث يكون قطب العالم على سمت الرأس وذلك موضعان معينان على وجه الأرض ( ينطبق المعدل على الأفق لاتحاد قطبيهما ولكون محوره) أي محور المعدل المستقيم الواصل بين قطبيه ماراً بمركزه ( قائماً على سطح (الأفق ) هناك (تكون الحركة اليومية فيه رحوبة ويكون النصف من منطقة البروج ) وهو الواقع من المعدل في جهة القطب الظاهر ( فوق الأرض دائماً والنصف الآخر منها (تحته دائماً ) . ولا تكون هناك للكواكب ولا لشيء من النقط المفروضة على الفلك طلوع ولا غروب بحركة الكل بل بحركاتها الخاصة ( فتكون السنة كلها يوماً وليلة) لأن مدة قطع الشمس بحركتها النصف الظاهر من البروج نهار ومدة قطعها النصف الخفي ليل وهاتان المدتان تتفاوتان بسبب الأوج والحضيض. فالنهار تحت القطب الشمالي أطول من الليل وتحت القطب الجنوبي أقصر ) إلا أن الشمس تدور ) بحركة الكل (في أربع وعشرين ساعة من موازة نقطة معينة من الأفق الذي هو المعدل ( إلى أن تعود إلى مثلها تلك الموازاة لتلك النقطة . ( وتزداد ) الشمس ( ارتفاعا ) عن الأفق ( في ثلاثة أشهر) ويكون غاية ارتفاعها بمقدار الميل الكلي (و) تزداد (انحطاطا) عن الارتفاع نحو الأفق ( في ثلاثة أشهر أخرى أيضاً ( حتى تغرب وتكون تحت الأرض ستة أشهر كذلك ) أي يزداد انحطاطها عن الأفق في ثلاثة أشهر إلى غاية الانحطاط التي هي الميل الكلي ثم ترتفع عنها في ثلاثة أشهر أخرى حتى تصل إلى الأفق .
It reaches the pole: the daily motion is conjugate and its horizons are called inclined. And where the pole of the world is at the zenith, and those are two specific places on the face of the Earth, the equator coincides with the horizon due to the union of their poles and because its axis, i.e., the straight axis of the equator extending between its poles passing through its center, is perpendicular to the surface of the horizon. There, the daily motion is spacious, and half of the ecliptic, which is the part of the equator in the direction of the apparent pole, is always above the Earth, and the other half is always below it. And there is for the planets and for any of the assumed points on the celestial sphere no rising or setting by the daily motion, but by their own motions. So the entire year becomes day and night, because the duration of the sun's cutting by its motion the apparent half of the ecliptic is daytime, and the duration of its cutting the hidden half is night, and these two durations differ due to apogee and perigee. So daytime under the North Pole is longer than night, and under the South Pole it is shorter. However, the sun rotates by the daily motion in twenty-four hours from being in line with a specific point on the horizon which is the equator until it returns to the same alignment for that point. And the sun increases in height above the horizon in three months, and its maximum height is to the extent of the total declination; and it increases in declination from the height towards the horizon in another three months until it sets and is under the Earth for six months likewise; i.e., its declination below the horizon increases in three months to the extreme declination which is the total declination, then it rises from it in another three months until it reaches the horizon.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage describes the astronomical conditions at the Earth's poles. The commentator explains that at the North and South Poles, the celestial equator aligns with the horizon because the Earth's axis is perpendicular. Consequently, the daily motion of the celestial sphere is such that no star rises or sets by that daily motion; instead, stars only rise and set due to their own proper motions. The ecliptic is split: one half is always above the horizon and the other always below, leading to a year that consists of one long day and one long night, each of six months. The duration of day and night at the poles differs due to the eccentricity of the sun's orbit (apogee and perigee), with the North Pole having a longer day and the South Pole a shorter day. The sun moves in a daily circle parallel to the horizon, taking 24 hours to return to the same alignment. Over the year, the sun's altitude changes gradually: it rises to its maximum (equal to the total declination of the ecliptic, about 23.5 degrees) over three months, then descends over three months until it sets, remaining below the horizon for six months, then rises again over three months. This explains the polar day and night cycle.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: At the poles, day and night are exactly 6 months each and equal in duration.
Correction: The passage states that due to apogee and perigee (eccentricity of the sun's orbit), daytime at the North Pole is longer than night, and at the South Pole it is shorter. So they are not exactly equal.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
At the poles, the celestial equator aligns with the horizon, causing the daily motion to not produce rising/setting; the sun's annual shift along the ecliptic results in a six-month day and six-month night, with slight variations due to orbital eccentricity.

Cause of Dawn and Twilight
Explains the cause of dawn and twilight as the illumination of vapor by the sun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the physical causes of dawn and twilight as described in the text, including the role of the vapor sphere and the Earth's shadow cone.

Core Idea
Dawn and twilight are caused by sunlight illuminating vapor, not by direct sunlight.

This passage from the text explains the natural phenomena of dawn and twilight using the physics of vapor and the Earth's shadow. It describes how the sun's position relative to the horizon and the vapor sphere create the gradual light changes we observe. The author also references the shadow cone to explain the reddish hues seen at dawn and dusk.

1

The vapor sphere 'condenses by light' – likely means becomes visible.

سبب الصبح كرة البخار تتكثف بالضوء لأنها تقبل نور الشمس كما تقدم في آخر مباحث المبصرات فإذا قربت الشمس من الأفق في جانب الشرق ولم يبق من قوس انحطاطها إلا مقدار ثماني عشرة درجة على ما عرف بالتجربة استنار بضوئها البخار الكثيف الواقع في ذلك الجانب فيرى ذلك النور المتزايد بزيادة قرب الشمس وهو الصبح . والشفق مثله لكنه عكسه في أن أوله كآخر الصبح وآخره كأوله هذا ما يليق بالكتاب وأما تصويرهما على ما ينبغي فليطلب من موضع آخر . والحمرة التي قوله : ( فليطلب من مواضع أخر اعلم أنه إذا كانت الشمس على نصف النهار من تحت كان مخروط ظل الأرض حينئذ قائماً على سطح الأرض في نصف النهار من فوق ولم يكن ذلك المخروط مائلاً إلى جانب أصلاً . ثم إذا قربت الشمس إلى الأفق الشرقي من تحت مال ذلك المخروط إلى جهة المغرب من فوق فيكون المرئي أولاً من الشعاع المحيط بذلك المخروط ما هو
The cause of dawn: the sphere of vapor condenses by light because it receives the light of the sun as previously mentioned at the end of the discussions of visible things. So when the sun approaches the horizon in the eastern side and there remains of its arc of declination only the amount of eighteen degrees, as known by experience, the dense vapor located in that side is illuminated by its light, and that increasing light is seen as the sun approaches, and it is dawn. And twilight is similar to it, but it is its opposite in that its beginning is like the end of dawn and its end is like its beginning. This is what befits the book, and as for their depiction as it should be, let it be sought from another place. And the redness which... The commentator says: 'Let it be sought from other places: Know that when the sun is at noon below, the cone of the Earth's shadow is then standing upon the surface of the Earth at noon above, and that cone was not inclined to any side at all. Then when the sun approaches the eastern horizon from below, that cone inclines towards the western side above, so the first thing seen from the ray surrounding that cone is what...'
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage provides the physical explanation for dawn and twilight. The author states that the cause of dawn is the condensation of the vapor sphere by light, because vapor receives sunlight. When the sun is near the eastern horizon but still below it, and only eighteen degrees of its arc of depression remain (as determined by experience), the dense vapor in that direction is illuminated, producing a gradually increasing light seen as dawn. Twilight is similar but opposite in sequence: the beginning of twilight matches the end of dawn and vice versa. The author then notes that a full depiction should be sought elsewhere. The commentator adds that when the sun is below the meridian, the Earth's shadow cone is upright; as the sun approaches the eastern horizon from below, the cone tilts westward, so the first visible ray is from the periphery of that cone. This explains the reddish hues often seen at dawn and dusk.

The Cause of Dawn and Twilight
Definition of dawn and twilight and their cause via vapor sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the physical cause of dawn and twilight according to Islamic natural philosophy, specifically the role of the vapor sphere and sunlight.

Core Idea
Dawn and twilight are caused by sunlight illuminating the vapor sphere near the horizon.

In this passage, the author explains the natural phenomena of dawn and twilight based on the concept of a vapor sphere that receives sunlight. He connects the timing of these events to the sun's position relative to the horizon. This explanation is grounded in earlier discussions about visible objects and empirical observations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to understand the overall explanation.
Notice the reference to eighteen degrees and the vapor sphere.
2
Identify the cause of dawn

The sun must be about eighteen degrees below the horizon.

Understand the analogy for twilight

This is due to the sun's movement away from the horizon.

This shows reliance on observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سبب الصبح كرة البخار تتكثف بالضوء لأنها تقبل نور الشمس كما تقدم ( في آخر مباحث المبصرات فإذا قربت الشمس من الأفق في جانب الشرق ولم يبق من قوس انحطاطها إلا مقدار ثماني عشرة درجة على ما عرف بالتجربة استنار بضوئها البخار الكثيف الواقع في ذلك الجانب فيرى ذلك النور المتزايد بزيادة قرب الشمس وهو الصبح ( والشفق مثله ( لكنه عكسه في أن أوله كآخر الصبح وآخره كأوله هذا ما يليق بالكتاب وأما تصويرهما على ما ينبغي فليطلب من موضع آخر ( والحمرة التي توجد في أول الشفق وآخر الصبح إنما هي ( لتكاثف الأبخرة في الأفق وزيادة سمكها بالنسبة إلى الباصرة (لأنها أي تلك الزيادة في غلظ الأبخرة ( بقدر ربع دور الأرض ) كما يظهر بالتخيل الصادق ( وتنقص ) تلك الزيادة ) في غيرها ) أي غير دائرة الأفق شيئاً فشيئاً ( حتى يكون ) تكاثف الأبخرة ( بقدر غلظ البخار) كما بالنسبة إلى سمت الرأس ) وقد ذكر أنه اعتبرها أي كرة البخار ( المهندسون فوجدوها ) أي غلظها ( ستة عشر فرسخاً) أو سبعة عشر.
The cause of dawn is the sphere of vapor that becomes dense by light because it receives the light of the sun, as previously mentioned in the last investigations of visible objects. So when the sun approaches the horizon on the eastern side and nothing remains of its arc of depression except about eighteen degrees, as known by experience, the thick vapor located in that side is illuminated by its light, and that light is seen, increasing with the increase of the sun's proximity, and it is dawn. And twilight is similar, but it is its opposite in that its beginning is like the end of dawn and its end is like its beginning. This is what is appropriate for the book; as for their depiction as it should be, let it be sought from another place. And the redness that appears at the beginning of twilight and the end of dawn is only due to the condensation of vapors in the horizon and the increase of their thickness relative to the observer—because that increase in the thickness of the vapors is by about a quarter of the circumference of the earth, as appears by true imagination—and that increase decreases elsewhere, i.e., other than the circle of the horizon, little by little, until the condensation of vapors becomes like the thickness of the vapor as relative to the zenith. And it has been mentioned that the engineers considered it, i.e., the sphere of vapor, and found it, i.e., its thickness, to be sixteen or seventeen leagues.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the natural phenomena of dawn and twilight. The author begins by stating that the cause of dawn is the sphere of vapor (كرة البخار) that becomes dense when illuminated by sunlight. This is based on earlier discussions about visible objects. He then explains that when the sun is about eighteen degrees below the horizon (as known from experience), the thick vapor in the eastern direction is illuminated, and the increasing light is perceived as dawn. Twilight is similar but opposite: its beginning resembles the end of dawn and vice versa. The author notes that a fuller depiction should be sought elsewhere. Next, he addresses the redness seen at the beginning of twilight and the end of dawn, attributing it to the condensation of vapors in the horizon and their increased thickness relative to the observer. This increase in thickness is about a quarter of the Earth's circumference (presumably along the horizon circle), as confirmed by true imagination. The thickness decreases as one moves away from the horizon towards the zenith. Finally, the author mentions that engineers have measured the thickness of the vapor sphere and found it to be sixteen or seventeen leagues.

Correction: Dawn is caused by sunlight illuminating the vapor sphere, not direct sunlight.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Dawn and twilight are caused by sunlight illuminating the vapor sphere near the horizon, with the sun about 18 degrees below.

The Redness of Twilight and Dawn and the Thickness of the Vapor Sphere
Explanation of redness and measurement of vapor sphere thickness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reason for the redness at dawn and twilight and the measured thickness of the vapor sphere according to classical Islamic scholars.

Core Idea
Redness at twilight and dawn is due to increased vapor thickness near the horizon, measured as 16-17 leagues.

In the second part of the passage, the author explains the reddish color observed at the beginning of twilight and the end of dawn. He attributes it to the condensation and increased thickness of vapors near the horizon. He also provides a measurement of the vapor sphere's thickness as determined by engineers, giving a concrete sense of the scale.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on redness
Read the Arabic and translation for the explanation of redness.
Notice the terms for condensation and thickness.
2
Understand the cause of redness
Redness is due to condensation of vapors in the horizon and increase of their thickness relative to the observer.
The author says this increase is about a quarter of Earth's circumference.
3
Examine the variation in thickness
The thickness decreases away from the horizon towards the zenith.
This explains why redness is only near the horizon.
4
Learn the engineers' measurement
Engineers measured the vapor sphere's thickness as 16 or 17 leagues.
A league (فرسخ) is about 5.5 km.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سبب الصبح كرة البخار تتكثف بالضوء لأنها تقبل نور الشمس كما تقدم ( في آخر مباحث المبصرات فإذا قربت الشمس من الأفق في جانب الشرق ولم يبق من قوس انحطاطها إلا مقدار ثماني عشرة درجة على ما عرف بالتجربة استنار بضوئها البخار الكثيف الواقع في ذلك الجانب فيرى ذلك النور المتزايد بزيادة قرب الشمس وهو الصبح ( والشفق مثله ( لكنه عكسه في أن أوله كآخر الصبح وآخره كأوله هذا ما يليق بالكتاب وأما تصويرهما على ما ينبغي فليطلب من موضع آخر ( والحمرة التي توجد في أول الشفق وآخر الصبح إنما هي ( لتكاثف الأبخرة في الأفق وزيادة سمكها بالنسبة إلى الباصرة (لأنها أي تلك الزيادة في غلظ الأبخرة ( بقدر ربع دور الأرض ) كما يظهر بالتخيل الصادق ( وتنقص ) تلك الزيادة ) في غيرها ) أي غير دائرة الأفق شيئاً فشيئاً ( حتى يكون ) تكاثف الأبخرة ( بقدر غلظ البخار) كما بالنسبة إلى سمت الرأس ) وقد ذكر أنه اعتبرها أي كرة البخار ( المهندسون فوجدوها ) أي غلظها ( ستة عشر فرسخاً) أو سبعة عشر.
The cause of dawn is the sphere of vapor that becomes dense by light because it receives the light of the sun, as previously mentioned in the last investigations of visible objects. So when the sun approaches the horizon on the eastern side and nothing remains of its arc of depression except about eighteen degrees, as known by experience, the thick vapor located in that side is illuminated by its light, and that light is seen, increasing with the increase of the sun's proximity, and it is dawn. And twilight is similar, but it is its opposite in that its beginning is like the end of dawn and its end is like its beginning. This is what is appropriate for the book; as for their depiction as it should be, let it be sought from another place. And the redness that appears at the beginning of twilight and the end of dawn is only due to the condensation of vapors in the horizon and the increase of their thickness relative to the observer—because that increase in the thickness of the vapors is by about a quarter of the circumference of the earth, as appears by true imagination—and that increase decreases elsewhere, i.e., other than the circle of the horizon, little by little, until the condensation of vapors becomes like the thickness of the vapor as relative to the zenith. And it has been mentioned that the engineers considered it, i.e., the sphere of vapor, and found it, i.e., its thickness, to be sixteen or seventeen leagues.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the natural phenomena of dawn and twilight. The author begins by stating that the cause of dawn is the sphere of vapor (كرة البخار) that becomes dense when illuminated by sunlight. This is based on earlier discussions about visible objects. He then explains that when the sun is about eighteen degrees below the horizon (as known from experience), the thick vapor in the eastern direction is illuminated, and the increasing light is perceived as dawn. Twilight is similar but opposite: its beginning resembles the end of dawn and vice versa. The author notes that a fuller depiction should be sought elsewhere. Next, he addresses the redness seen at the beginning of twilight and the end of dawn, attributing it to the condensation of vapors in the horizon and their increased thickness relative to the observer. This increase in thickness is about a quarter of the Earth's circumference (presumably along the horizon circle), as confirmed by true imagination. The thickness decreases as one moves away from the horizon towards the zenith. Finally, the author mentions that engineers have measured the thickness of the vapor sphere and found it to be sixteen or seventeen leagues.

The Geometry of Dawn and Twilight: The Earth's Shadow Cone
Commentator explains the geometry of the earth's shadow cone and the visibility of dawn and twilight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the geometry of the earth's shadow cone explains the sequence of false dawn, true dawn, and twilight, and the empirical basis for the 18-degree solar depression.

Core Idea
The shadow cone tilts as the sun approaches, creating the sequence of dawn lights.

The commentator builds on earlier discussions by providing a geometric model of dawn and twilight. By tracing the sun's position relative to the earth's shadow, he shows how the false dawn appears as a narrow strip of light before the true dawn spreads across the horizon. This passage also clarifies the empirical basis for the 18-degree solar depression used in determining prayer times.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let the commentator's own words establish the geometry before you analyze it.
2
Visualize the shadow cone tilting
Imagine the earth's shadow cone at midnight standing upright. As the sun approaches the eastern horizon from below, the cone tilts westward. The side closest to the sun becomes visible.
The side facing the sun is the eastern side at dawn.
3
Distinguish false dawn from true dawn
The first light seen is from the side of the cone nearest the sun, but it does not reach the horizon because the light has not yet spread. This is false dawn. When the sun gets very close, light spreads and illuminates the horizon, becoming true dawn.
False dawn appears as a vertical shaft; true dawn spreads horizontally.
4
Apply the same reasoning to twilight
Twilight is the opposite of dawn: at dusk, the sun is below the western horizon, and the shadow cone tilts eastward. The same geometry applies in reverse.
The commentator says 'analogize' and 'reflect'.
5
Connect the empirical measurement of 18 degrees
Experience shows that the sun is 18 degrees below the horizon at the start of dawn and end of twilight. This measurement is used in defining the times for prayer and fasting.
The 'quarter of the earth's circumference' refers to the observer's view of the horizon arc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فليطلب من مواضع أخر اعلم أنه إذا كانت الشمس على نصف النهار من تحت كان مخروط ظل الأرض حينئذ قائماً على سطح الأرض في نصف النهار من فوق ولم يكن ذلك المخروط مائلاً إلى جانب أصلاً. ثم إذا قربت الشمس إلى الأفق الشرقي من تحت مال، ذلك المخروط إلى جهة المغرب من فوق فيكون المرئي أولاً من الشعاع المحيط بذلك المخروط ما هو أقرب إلى البصر. ولا شك أن الأقرب إلى البصر من جوانب المخروط وهو الجانب الذي يلي الشمس وهو الجانب الشرقي في الصبح فإن جانب الغرب في غاية البعد عن البصر حينئذ . ثم إن الجانب المرئي إلى قرب الذي يلي الشمس لا تكون أولاً متصلاً بالأفق إذا لم يكن النور أولاً منبسطاً على وجه الأرض ولم تكن الخطوط الشعاعية البصرية نافذة إلى منتهى الأفق. فإذن يكون أول ما يرى من نور الشمس هو ما يرى فوق الأفق بخط مستقيم ناشئ من الشمس، وحينئذ يكون ما يقرب من الأفق مظلماً بعد فلذلك يسمى ذلك النور بالصبح الأول وبالصبح الكاذب أيضاً إذا قربت الشمس جداً انبسط النور فصار الأفق مستنيراً ويصير الصبح صادقاً، وقس على هذا حال الشفق لكنه يكون بعكس الصبح فتأمل واعلم أنه قد علم بالتجربة أن انحطاط الشمس من الأرض عند أول طلوع الصبح وآخر غروب الشفق يكون ثمانية عشر جزءاً من أجزاء البروج كما ذكر في موضعه وقوله بقد ربع دور الأرض وهو من الناظر إلى الأفق الشرقي في الصبح ومن الناظر إلى الأفق الغربي في الشفق فيكون مجموعها بقدر نصف دور الأرض. ولا شك أن الأبخرة التي كانت في جانب المشرقي أو في جانب المغرب ترى أكثر وأطول من الأبخرة التي كانت في جانب المشرق أو في جانب المغرب ترى أكثر وأطول من الأبخرة التي كانت في سمت الرأس أو قريبة منه كما يشهد به الإحساس والتخيل الصادق .
His saying: (So let it be sought from other places). Know that when the sun is at noon below, the cone of the earth's shadow at that time is standing on the surface of the earth at noon above, and that cone is not inclined to any side at all. Then when the sun approaches the eastern horizon from below, that cone inclines towards the west above, so what is first seen of the light surrounding that cone is that which is closer to the eye. There is no doubt that the closest to the eye of the sides of the cone is the side that faces the sun, which is the eastern side at dawn, for the western side is extremely far from the eye then. Then the visible side near that which faces the sun does not initially connect to the horizon if the light is not first spread over the face of the earth and the visual ray lines do not penetrate to the end of the horizon. So then the first thing seen of the sun's light is that which is seen above the horizon by a straight line arising from the sun, and at that time what is near the horizon is dark afterwards; therefore that light is called the first dawn and also the false dawn. When the sun gets very close, the light spreads and the horizon becomes illuminated, and the dawn becomes true. Analogize this to the case of twilight, but it is the opposite of dawn, so reflect. And know that it has been learned by experience that the depression of the sun from the earth at the first appearance of dawn and the last disappearance of twilight is eighteen parts of the zodiacal divisions, as mentioned in its place. And his saying 'by about a quarter of the circumference of the earth' refers to the observer facing the eastern horizon at dawn and the western horizon at twilight, so their total amounts to half the circumference of the earth. And there is no doubt that the vapors that are in the eastern side or the western side appear larger and longer than the vapors that are at the zenith or near it, as attested by sense-perception and true imagination.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the geometry behind the visibility of dawn and twilight. The commentator begins by describing the earth's shadow cone (مخروط ظل الأرض). When the sun is at its lowest point (midnight below), the shadow cone stands upright. As the sun approaches the eastern horizon, the cone tilts westward. The first light seen from the sun-illuminated cone is from its side closest to the observer—the eastern side at dawn. This light does not initially reach the horizon because the light has not yet spread across the Earth's surface and the visual rays do not extend to the far horizon. Thus, what is first seen is a narrow strip of light above the horizon, which is called the 'first dawn' or 'false dawn' (الصبح الكاذب). As the sun gets very close, the light spreads and illuminates the horizon, producing the 'true dawn' (الصبح الصادق). The same principle applies to twilight but in reverse order. The commentator then confirms that experience shows the sun is 18 degrees below the horizon at the beginning of dawn and end of twilight. He interprets the phrase 'quarter of the earth's circumference' as referring to the observer's view of the horizon arc at dawn and twilight, totaling half the circumference. Finally, he notes that vapors near the horizon appear larger and longer than those near the zenith due to perspective, as confirmed by sense and imagination.

Key Terms
مخروط ظل الأرض
الصبح الكاذب
الصبح الصادق
انحطاط الشمس
أجزاء البروج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The false dawn and true dawn are different in kind, not just in geometry.
Correction: The commentator shows they are merely different stages of the same geometric phenomenon: the false dawn is when light from the cone's side is seen without reaching the horizon, and the true dawn is when the light spreads to the horizon.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, when does the true dawn appear?
A) When the sun is 18 degrees below the horizon and light spreads across the horizon
B) When the sun rises
C) When the first light appears above the horizon
D) When the vapors disappear

Key Takeaway
The geometry of the earth's shadow cone, combined with the sun's depression angle, explains why false dawn appears as a vertical shaft of light before the horizon becomes illuminated for true dawn.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness the false dawn, do you ponder the precise geometry that distinguishes it from true dawn, and how does this precision affect your awareness of the Creator's perfection?
?
The observer's perspective causes the false dawn to appear as a narrow strip. In your spiritual life, what 'false dawns'—things that seem true but are not—do you need to examine more carefully based on the light of knowledge?
?
The source says the vapors near the horizon appear larger. When you look at the horizon of your own life, what appears large that may only be an illusion of distance? How can you align your vision with reality?

Understanding False Dawn, True Dawn, and Twilight
Jilani explains the cause of false and true dawn and twilight using vapor thickness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the physical cause of false dawn and true dawn, and how the thickness of vapors on the horizon creates these phenomena.

Core Idea
False dawn is a vapor effect, not celestial light – true dawn comes when sunlight spreads across the horizon.

Have you ever noticed a faint light before sunrise that disappears and then reappears more broadly? Medieval scholars understood this as a result of atmospheric vapors, not direct sunlight. In this passage, al-Jilani explains how the thickness of vapors on the horizon creates the false dawn, and why the true dawn only appears when the sun is close enough to illuminate the entire horizon.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full translation. Get a sense of the scientific and philosophical style.
This passage uses geometry and observation.
2
Identify the cause of false dawn
Look for the explanation of why the first light appears above the horizon while the horizon itself remains dark. Note the role of vapor thickness and the cone of light.
The false dawn is not direct sunlight.
3
Understand the difference between false and true dawn
Notice when the dawn becomes 'true' – when the light spreads and the horizon becomes illuminated. The text also compares twilight as the reverse.
4
Reflect on the measurements given
The author gives specific numbers: a quarter of Earth's diameter, 16-17 parasangs, 18 degrees. Think about how these were estimated and what they show about the reasoning process.
These approximations were based on empirical observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
توجد في أول الشفق وآخر الصبح إنما هي ( لتكاثف الأبخرة في الأفق وزيادة سمكها بالنسبة إلى الباصرة (لأنها أي تلك الزيادة في غلظ الأبخرة ( بقدر ربع دور الأرض ) كما يظهر بالتخيل الصادق ( وتنقص ) تلك الزيادة ) في غيرها ) أي غير دائرة الأفق شيئاً فشيئاً ( حتى يكون ) تكاثف الأبخرة ( بقدر غلظ البخار) كما بالنسبة إلى سمت الرأس ) وقد ذكر أنه اعتبرها أي كرة البخار ( المهندسون فوجدوها ) أي غلظها ( ستة عشر فرسخاً) أو سبعة عشر. ثم أقرب إلى البصر. ولا شك أن الأقرب إلى البصر من جوانب المخروط وهو الجانب الذي يلي الشمس وهو الجانب الشرقي في الصبح فإن جانب الغرب في غاية البعد عن البصر حينئذ. ثم إن الجانب المرئي إلى قرب الذي يلي الشمس لا تكون أولاً متصلاً بالأفق إذا لم يكن النور أولاً منبسطاً على وجه الأرض ولم تكن الخطوط الشعاعية البصرية نافذة إلى منتهى الأفق. فإذن يكون أول ما يرى من نور الشمس هو ما يرى فوق الأفق بخط مستقيم ناشئ من الشمس، وحينئذ يكون ما يقرب من الأفق مظلماً بعد فلذلك يسمى ذلك النور بالصبح الأول وبالصبح الكاذب أيضاً إذا قربت الشمس جداً انبسط النور فصار الأفق مستنيراً ويصير الصبح صادقاً، وقس على هذا حال الشفق لكنه يكون بعكس الصبح فتأمل. واعلم أنه قد علم بالتجربة أن انحطاط الشمس من الأرض عند أول طلوع الصبح وآخر غروب الشفق يكون ثمانية عشر جزءاً من أجزاء البروج كما ذكر في موضعه وقوله بقدر ربع دور الأرض وهو من الناظر إلى الأفق الشرقي في الصبح ومن الناظر إلى الأفق الغربي في الشفق فيكون مجموعها بقدر نصف دور الأرض. ولا شك أن الأبخرة التي كانت في جانب المشرقي أو في جانب المغرب ترى أكثر وأطول من الأبخرة التي كانت في سمت الرأس أو قريبة منه كما يشهد به الإحساس والتخيل الصادق.
They occur at the beginning of twilight and the end of dawn only due to the thickening of vapors on the horizon and the increase of their thickness relative to the eye, because that increase in the thickness of the vapors [is] by a quarter of the earth's diameter, as appears by true imagination. And that increase decreases elsewhere, i.e., other than the circle of the horizon, little by little, until the thickening of the vapors is as much as the thickness of vapor relative to the zenith. And it has been mentioned that the engineers considered it, i.e., the sphere of vapor, and found it, i.e., its thickness, sixteen parasangs or seventeen. Then [it is] closer to the eye. There is no doubt that the closest to the eye of the sides of the cone is the side that faces the sun, and it is the eastern side at dawn, for the western side is extremely far from the eye at that time. Then, the visible side close to that which faces the sun is not initially connected to the horizon if the light is not first spread over the surface of the earth and the visual rays do not penetrate to the end of the horizon. Thus, the first thing seen of the sun's light is what is seen above the horizon by a straight line arising from the sun, and then what is near the horizon is dark afterwards; therefore that light is called the first dawn and also the false dawn. When the sun comes very close, the light spreads and the horizon becomes illuminated, and the dawn becomes true. And apply this to the case of twilight, but it is the opposite of dawn, so reflect. And know that it has been known by experience that the depression of the sun from the earth at the first rising of dawn and the last setting of twilight is eighteen parts of the zodiacal signs, as mentioned in its place, and his statement 'by a quarter of the earth's diameter' – it is from the observer to the eastern horizon at dawn and from the observer to the western horizon at twilight, so their total is half the earth's diameter. There is no doubt that the vapors that were on the eastern side or on the western side are seen as more and longer than the vapors that were at the zenith or near it, as attested by sensation and true imagination.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage explains the astronomical phenomena of false dawn (al-ṣubḥ al-kādhib), true dawn (al-ṣubḥ al-ṣādiq), and twilight (al-shafaq). It begins by stating that the light seen at the beginning of twilight and at the end of dawn is due to the thickening of vapors on the horizon. The thickness of these vapors relative to the eye is quantified as a quarter of the earth's diameter at the horizon, decreasing gradually toward the zenith. Engineers have measured the vapor sphere's thickness as sixteen or seventeen parasangs. The explanation then turns to why the false dawn appears: the side of the cone of light closest to the sun (the eastern side at dawn) is closest to the eye, while the western side is farthest. The light is not initially spread across the earth's surface, so the first visible sunlight appears above the horizon in a straight line, leaving the area near the horizon dark. This is the false dawn. When the sun approaches, the light spreads and illuminates the horizon, becoming the true dawn. The same applies to twilight in reverse. Finally, the author notes that experience shows the sun's depression at dawn and twilight is 18 degrees, and the sum of the eastern and western horizon thicknesses equals half the earth's diameter. The vapors at the horizon are thicker than those at the zenith, as confirmed by observation and imagination.

Correction: The false dawn is actually caused by the thickening of vapors on the horizon, not by any celestial object.

The Six Transformations of the Elements and the Unity of Matter
Enumeration of element transformations and argument for unity of matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six direct transformations among the four elements, the conditions for needing intermediates, and how this supports the conclusion that all elements share a single underlying matter.

Core Idea
The transformations of the elements reveal a single matter prepared for all forms.

This passage from the commentary on al-Mawaqif examines how the four elements (fire, air, water, earth) transform into each other. The author first lists six transformations that occur directly when elements share one quality, then explains when intermediates are required. From this analysis, he concludes that the underlying matter (hayula) is one and the same, with differences arising from proximity to the celestial sphere. The passage also includes an objection and the author's skepticism about the very premises of matter and form.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Focus on the enumeration of transformations and the key terms: انقلاب, هيولى, عناصر.
The passage is technical; take your time.
2
Identify the six direct transformations
Find where the author lists six transformations without an intermediate. Note which elements are involved and why they transform directly (sharing one quality).
The author says 'فهذه ست انقلابات بلا وسط'.
3
Understand the role of intermediates
Read the part about transformations requiring an intermediate when elements differ in both qualities. See the example of water and fire, and the resolution about earth and fire.
The author offers a rule based on adjacency.
4
Grasp the conclusion about matter
The author states that all these transformations indicate one matter (hayula) receptive to all elemental forms. Consider how qualities vary with nearness to the celestial sphere.
5
Reflect on the objection
The passage ends with an objection: if matter is one, then differences need an external cause. Also, the author rejects the composition of bodies from matter and form and the transformations themselves.
This shows the author's critical stance.
